Refutation of the Dimension View: The First Argument
Al-Jurjani refutes the dimension view by proving its impossibility through motion and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the view that place is a dimension, based on the impossibility of dimension accepting spatial motion due to infinite regress.

Core Idea
If place were a dimension, it would accept motion, leading to an infinite regress of places.

After presenting the two possibilities, Al-Jurjani argues that place cannot be a dimension. He divides the refutation into two parts: the supposed dimension (already proven impossible) and the existing dimension. For the existing dimension, he gives two arguments. In this lesson, we examine the first argument, which shows that if dimension accepts motion, it leads to an infinite regress and a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the division: supposed vs. existing dimension
Notice that the refutation is split into two: the supposed dimension (already dealt with) and the existing dimension.
The supposed dimension is referenced as already proven by four aspects.
2
Understand the first argument's dichotomy
The existing dimension either accepts spatial motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya) by its essence or it does not. Both options are examined.
'Accepts' here means 'is possible for it'.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If dimension accepts motion, it would move from place to place, requiring another place, leading to an infinite chain of interpenetrating dimensions.
An infinite regress is considered impossible in this context.
4
Note the contradiction
The infinite set of places, if all transferred, would produce a place that is both inside and outside the set, a logical contradiction.
The marginal comment clarifies that this follows from the transfer of each individual place.

قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) وذلك لأن المراد بخروج كل واحد خروج كل بعد سواء كان مجتمعاً مع آخر أولا فإذا خرج كل واحد بهذا المعنى خرج الكل وليس المراد خروج كل واحد بشرط الانفراد عن الآخر حتى لا يستلزم الحكم على كل واحد الحكم على الكل، كما في قولنا كل رجل يشبعه هذا الرغيف .
The second observation - the ninth objective: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. The surrounding [surface] until if a shield is placed on the head of a dome to the extent of a dirham, they only considered its place to be the amount that prevents it from descending. The first possibility: that it (i.e., place) is the inner surface of the container tangent to the outer surface of the contained, and this is the doctrine of Aristotle. The later philosophers such as Ibn Sina and al-Farabi and their followers are upon it. Otherwise, i.e., if place were not the surface, it would be dimension, as it has passed that it does not exit from them. And that it, i.e., its being a dimension, is impossible. As for the supposed dimension, it has passed that it exists by the four aspects indicating that. And as for the existing dimension, it is [refuted] by two arguments. The first: that that dimension either accepts motion in space (al-haraka al-ayniyya) by its essence or does not accept it. Both divisions are false. As for the first, because if the dimension accepted spatial motion, then from place to place, for there is no meaning of spatial motion except transition. Then after that it has another place and it leads to an infinite regress, and there would be infinite dimensions interpenetrating each other. And that is necessarily impossible. And how could it not be impossible? And all those places, inasmuch as they are all, all of them can be transferred. Because if the transfer of each one is possible, then the transfer of the whole inasmuch as it is a whole is also possible. Do you not see that if each one leaves its place, then the whole has left. So it, i.e., the whole, has a place. That place is inside those places because it is one of them, and outside them because it is a container for them. This is a contradiction because it combines two opposites. His saying: 'Either it accepts motion by its essence' – acceptance may be used in the sense of possibility, as it is said: essence accepts existence and non-existence by its essence, and that is intended here. That is, the dimension either can have motion with respect to its essence, or cannot have it with respect to its essence, and there is no middle ground between the two. On the first, an infinite regress follows; on the second, it prevents it from being described by motion. So it is not objected that if by its not accepting it is meant that its essence necessitates non-acceptance, then the dichotomy is not exhaustive for it is possible that it does not necessitate acceptance nor its opposite. And if what is intended is that it is not described with acceptance with respect to its essence, then we do not grant the necessity of the impossibility of the body's acceptance of motion, because it is possible that the dimension is capable of it by following the body, even if it is not capable of it by its essence. His saying: 'Because if it accepted motion etc.' – its summary is that if motion were possible for it, then place would be possible for it, and if place were possible for it, then the impossible would be possible, which is the existence of infinite dimensions. Or it is said: if it were possible, then from the assumption of its occurrence an impossibility would not follow with respect to its essence, but the impossibility follows, so it repels what is imagined that acceptance of motion does not necessitate the actual occurrence of motions such that it would have another place, rather the possibility of place [is sufficient], and that does not necessitate an infinite regress. The descent, and it is impossible, for we witness each of them moving, and motion must be from something to something, and what the transition is from and to is place, as in al-Abkar. His saying: 'Even if a shield is placed' – a shield (darqa) is a shield made of leather without wood or sinew. His saying: 'By the four aspects indicating that' – he only attributed indication to them because they are reminders of the existence of place. Otherwise, it has preceded that what is beneficial for that is rational necessity. His saying: 'As for the first, because if the dimension accepted motion etc.' – it is answered by choosing the first branch and denying the necessity of infinite regress, because acceptance of motion is an expression of the possibility of being described by transition from place to place, and this possibility necessitates the possibility that the described has a place, not its necessity, so infinite regress does not follow. But there is consideration in this; because the necessity of infinite regress and the realization of infinite dimensions is not due to the description of each dimension with spatial motion actually, such that what was mentioned would be refuted, but rather because that which is capable of spatial motion must be something actually occupying place. For what is not related to place, like abstract entities, is not capable of it at all, and the opponent also acknowledges this. And the commentator will explicitly state in… His saying: 'Do you not see etc.' – that is because what is meant by each one leaving is the leaving of each dimension, whether it is combined with another or not. So if each one leaves in this sense, the whole leaves. What is not intended is each one leaving under the condition of being separate from the other, such that the judgment on each one does not entail the judgment on the whole, as in our statement: every man, this loaf satisfies him.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of place as the inner surface of the container (Aristotle's view) and begins refuting the alternative view that place is a dimension. The author first states the two possibilities: if place is not surface, it must be dimension. Then he argues that the dimension view is impossible. The proof is divided: for the supposed dimension (already proved impossible by four aspects) and for the existing dimension, which is refuted by two arguments. The passage covers the first argument in detail: either dimension accepts spatial motion or not. Both are false. If it accepts motion, it would need another place, leading to an infinite regress of interpenetrating dimensions, which is impossible. The author then explains the absurdity further: if all those places can be transferred, the whole has a place that is both inside and outside them—a contradiction. The marginal comments clarify the terminology: 'acceptance' means possibility, and the dichotomy is exhaustive. They also address an objection: possibility of motion does not necessitate actual motion, but the commentator argues that the capacity for motion requires actual occupancy of place, leading to infinite regress. The comment on 'Do you not see' explains that the whole's leaving follows from each part leaving, not under the condition of separateness.

الحركة الأينية
التسلسل
المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is an independent dimension that exists apart from the containing surface.
Correction: Place is the inner surface of the container, not a dimension. If it were a dimension, accepting motion would lead to infinite regress and contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what consequence follows if dimension accepts spatial motion?
A) It becomes identical to the body.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of dimensions.
C) It becomes immaterial.
D) It ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against the dimension view shows that if dimension accepts motion, an infinite regress and contradiction follow, proving it cannot be place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani shows that accepting a false premise leads to an infinite regress of contradictions. Is there a belief in your life about God or religion that you hold without following it to its logical conclusion? Are you willing to trace it back until you see whether it stands or falls?
?
The commentator argues that even the possibility of motion requires actual occupancy of place. In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that good intentions (possibility) are enough without actual commitment (occupying the place)? What would it mean for you to 'occupy' the truth you claim to believe, not just intend it?

Refutation of the Second Division (Motion Argument)
Al-Jurjani refutes the second division using a conditional argument based on observed motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second division of the dimension view is false because observed motion of bodies implies that dimension must accept motion.

Core Idea
If dimension could not move, bodies could not move—but they do, so dimension must accept motion.

Sanusi refutes the second division of the dimension view by showing that it leads to a contradiction with observation. He argues that if dimension cannot move, then bodies containing it cannot move, but we plainly see bodies moving. Thus, the premise that dimension does not accept motion is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the logical flow.
Note the conditional structure: if dimension does not accept motion, then body does not accept motion.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage uses modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. P is 'dimension does not accept motion', Q is 'body does not accept motion'.
3
Connect to observation
The observation that bodies move (not Q) is the key. Ask yourself: does this observation truly force the conclusion?
This refutes the second division because the impossibility of dimension moving is negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني: فلأن البعد إذا لم يقبل الحركة لذاته فالجسم أيضاً لا يقبلها لما فيه من البعد فإن حركة الجسم مستلزمة لحركة البعد الحال فيه فامتناع حركة البعد مستلزم لامتناع حركة الجسم واللازم وهو عدم قبول الجسم الحركة باطل بالمشاهدة الدالة على قبوله إياها فكذا الملزوم وهو عدم قبول البعد للحركة باطل . الوجه الثاني: أنه لو كان المكان هو البعد
قوله : ( فلأن البعد إذا لم يقبل الحركة لذاته أي لم يمكن له الحركة نظراً إلى ذاته على ما مر .
الإلهيات، بأنَّ المكان لا يمكن حصوله إلا في المكان. ولهذا استدل المحققون على أن الله تعالى ليس بمكاني بأنه لو كان كذلك لزم قدم المكان والجملة مبنى الكلام على أنه يستحيل أن يكون شيء في بعض أحيان وجوده مما لا تعلق له بالمكان وفي بعض منها متمكناً والظاهر أنَّ العقلاء متفقون عليه نعم يمكن أن ينقض الدليل بالبعد المجرد فإنه إن قبلها لذاته لزم التسلسل وإلا فسائر الأجسام لا يقبله أيضاً، فما هو جواب القائلين بالسطح فهو جواب القائلين بالبعد .
قوله : ( لأنه إذا أمكن انتقال كل واحد قد يمنع الشرطية بناء على أن إمكان كل درجة في نفسه لا ينافي امتناع الكل كما أشرنا إليه فيما سبق .
قوله : ( ألا ترى أنه إذا خرج كل واحد عن مكانه فإن قلت : خروج كل من الأجزاء الفلكية مكانه لا يستلزم خروج المجموع، فما الفرق بينه وبين ما نحن فيه . قلت : خروج كل جزء فيما نحن فيه إلى مكان غير مكان جزء آخر بالضرورة لتتطابق الأمكنة وتداخل الأبعاد على الفرض ولا كذلك فيما ذكرته فهذا هو منشأ الفرق فليتأمل .
قوله: (فالجسم أيضاً لايقبلها لما فيه من (البعد فإن قلت عدم قبول الحال في الجسم الحركة لذاته لا يستلزم عدم قبول الجسم إياها ألا ترى أن العرض الحال فيه لايقبلها لذاته، ولو
And as for the second division: because if the dimension does not accept motion by its essence, then the body also does not accept it because it contains dimension. For the motion of the body necessitates the motion of the dimension inherent in it. So the impossibility of the motion of the dimension necessitates the impossibility of the motion of the body. The consequent, which is the body's non-acceptance of motion, is false by observation indicating its acceptance of it. Thus the antecedent, which is the dimension's non-acceptance of motion, is false. The second argument: that if place were the dimension... His saying: 'Because if the dimension does not accept motion by its essence' - meaning it cannot have motion with respect to its essence, as has passed. The divine [science]: that place cannot occur except in place. For this reason the verifiers argued that God Most High is not spatial because if He were, the eternity of place would be necessary. The summary of the discourse is that it is impossible for something at some moments of its existence to have no relation to place and at others to be occupying place. And it is apparent that the rational people agree on it. Yes, it is possible to refute the argument by abstract dimension, for if it accepts motion by its essence, infinite regress follows, and if not, then other bodies do not accept it either. So what is the answer of those who hold the surface view is the answer of those who hold the dimension view. His saying: 'Because if the transfer of each one is possible' - he may deny the conditional based on the fact that the possibility of each degree in itself does not contradict the impossibility of the whole, as we indicated previously. His saying: 'Do you not see that if each one leaves its place' - then you may say: the leaving of each of the celestial parts from its place does not necessitate the leaving of the whole. What is the difference between it and our case? I say: the leaving of each part in our case is to a place other than the place of another part necessarily, so that the places coincide and the dimensions interpenetrate by hypothesis. It is not so in what you mentioned. This is the source of the difference, so let it be considered. His saying: 'So the body also does not accept it because of what it contains of dimension' - then you may say: the non-acceptance of the inherent accident of motion by its essence does not necessitate the body's non-acceptance of it. Do you not see that the accident inherent in it does not accept it by its essence? And if...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage completes the first argument and begins the second. The first argument's second division states: if dimension does not accept motion by its essence, then the body containing it also cannot move, because body's motion requires dimension's motion. But we observe bodies moving, so the consequent is false; therefore the antecedent (dimension's non-acceptance) is also false. This refutes the second division. The author then introduces the second argument: if place were a dimension, then place would require another place, leading to infinite regress. The marginal comments explain terms: 'does not accept motion by its essence' means it cannot have motion essentially. Then a longer comment discusses the divine science: place cannot occur except in place, used to argue that God is not spatial. It also notes that the argument can be refuted by considering abstract dimension; the answer to surface proponents applies to dimension proponents. Another comment addresses an objection: the possibility of each part transferring does not imply the possibility of the whole transferring, similar to previous discussions. The difference is that in this case, each part goes to a different place, causing interpenetration. The final incomplete comment discusses the relationship between body and accident regarding motion.

البعد
حركة الجسم
المشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that dimension is inherently immobile because it is abstract, but the argument shows that if it were, bodies could not move.
Correction: The argument proves that dimension must accept motion because bodies, which contain dimension, are observed to move.

Checkpoint
In the second division refutation, what is the observed fact that disproves the premise?
A) That dimension is immaterial.
B) That bodies move.
C) That place is a dimension.
D) That infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Observed motion of bodies proves that dimension must accept motion, thereby refuting the second division of the dimension view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you trusted your own observation over a comfortable assumption? What keeps you from applying the same logical rigor to your beliefs about God?
?
This argument shows that even a philosophical premise must bow to observable reality. Is there any belief you hold about your deen that you have never tested against clear evidence? What would it cost you to test it?
?
The conclusion that dimension accepts motion may seem trivial, but it points to the coherence of creation. When you look at the everyday motion of your own body, do you see it as a sign of Allah's wisdom? How would your day change if you saw every movement as a proof of His design?

The Second Argument: Infinite Regress from Place Being Dimension
Al-Jurjani presents the second argument and its theological implications, along with a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the dimension view, which demonstrates that if place were a dimension, it would lead to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If place is dimension, it must be in another place, leading to infinite regress.

The second argument against the dimension view is that if place were a dimension, then place itself would need a place to exist, because dimensions cannot exist without a substratum. This leads to an infinite chain, which is absurd. The argument also touches on theological points about God's nonspatiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the logical structure of the argument.
The passage includes a theological application about God not being spatial.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If place is a dimension, then place must itself be located in a place, because dimensions require a locus. This leads to an infinite regress.
The regress is impossible, so the assumption is false.
3
Consider the refutation and response
The passage also mentions a possible refutation using abstract dimension and notes that the same answer given by surface proponents applies here.
This shows the depth of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني: فلأن البعد إذا لم يقبل الحركة لذاته فالجسم أيضاً لا يقبلها لما فيه من البعد فإن حركة الجسم مستلزمة لحركة البعد الحال فيه فامتناع حركة البعد مستلزم لامتناع حركة الجسم واللازم وهو عدم قبول الجسم الحركة باطل بالمشاهدة الدالة على قبوله إياها فكذا الملزوم وهو عدم قبول البعد للحركة باطل . الوجه الثاني: أنه لو كان المكان هو البعد
قوله : ( فلأن البعد إذا لم يقبل الحركة لذاته أي لم يمكن له الحركة نظراً إلى ذاته على ما مر .
الإلهيات، بأنَّ المكان لا يمكن حصوله إلا في المكان. ولهذا استدل المحققون على أن الله تعالى ليس بمكاني بأنه لو كان كذلك لزم قدم المكان والجملة مبنى الكلام على أنه يستحيل أن يكون شيء في بعض أحيان وجوده مما لا تعلق له بالمكان وفي بعض منها متمكناً والظاهر أنَّ العقلاء متفقون عليه نعم يمكن أن ينقض الدليل بالبعد المجرد فإنه إن قبلها لذاته لزم التسلسل وإلا فسائر الأجسام لا يقبله أيضاً، فما هو جواب القائلين بالسطح فهو جواب القائلين بالبعد .
قوله : ( لأنه إذا أمكن انتقال كل واحد قد يمنع الشرطية بناء على أن إمكان كل درجة في نفسه لا ينافي امتناع الكل كما أشرنا إليه فيما سبق .
قوله : ( ألا ترى أنه إذا خرج كل واحد عن مكانه فإن قلت : خروج كل من الأجزاء الفلكية مكانه لا يستلزم خروج المجموع، فما الفرق بينه وبين ما نحن فيه . قلت : خروج كل جزء فيما نحن فيه إلى مكان غير مكان جزء آخر بالضرورة لتتطابق الأمكنة وتداخل الأبعاد على الفرض ولا كذلك فيما ذكرته فهذا هو منشأ الفرق فليتأمل .
قوله: (فالجسم أيضاً لايقبلها لما فيه من (البعد فإن قلت عدم قبول الحال في الجسم الحركة لذاته لا يستلزم عدم قبول الجسم إياها ألا ترى أن العرض الحال فيه لايقبلها لذاته، ولو
And as for the second division: because if the dimension does not accept motion by its essence, then the body also does not accept it because it contains dimension. For the motion of the body necessitates the motion of the dimension inherent in it. So the impossibility of the motion of the dimension necessitates the impossibility of the motion of the body. The consequent, which is the body's non-acceptance of motion, is false by observation indicating its acceptance of it. Thus the antecedent, which is the dimension's non-acceptance of motion, is false. The second argument: that if place were the dimension... His saying: 'Because if the dimension does not accept motion by its essence' - meaning it cannot have motion with respect to its essence, as has passed. The divine [science]: that place cannot occur except in place. For this reason the verifiers argued that God Most High is not spatial because if He were, the eternity of place would be necessary. The summary of the discourse is that it is impossible for something at some moments of its existence to have no relation to place and at others to be occupying place. And it is apparent that the rational people agree on it. Yes, it is possible to refute the argument by abstract dimension, for if it accepts motion by its essence, infinite regress follows, and if not, then other bodies do not accept it either. So what is the answer of those who hold the surface view is the answer of those who hold the dimension view. His saying: 'Because if the transfer of each one is possible' - he may deny the conditional based on the fact that the possibility of each degree in itself does not contradict the impossibility of the whole, as we indicated previously. His saying: 'Do you not see that if each one leaves its place' - then you may say: the leaving of each of the celestial parts from its place does not necessitate the leaving of the whole. What is the difference between it and our case? I say: the leaving of each part in our case is to a place other than the place of another part necessarily, so that the places coincide and the dimensions interpenetrate by hypothesis. It is not so in what you mentioned. This is the source of the difference, so let it be considered. His saying: 'So the body also does not accept it because of what it contains of dimension' - then you may say: the non-acceptance of the inherent accident of motion by its essence does not necessitate the body's non-acceptance of it. Do you not see that the accident inherent in it does not accept it by its essence? And if...
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage completes the first argument and begins the second. The first argument's second division states: if dimension does not accept motion by its essence, then the body containing it also cannot move, because body's motion requires dimension's motion. But we observe bodies moving, so the consequent is false; therefore the antecedent (dimension's non-acceptance) is also false. This refutes the second division. The author then introduces the second argument: if place were a dimension, then place would require another place, leading to infinite regress. The marginal comments explain terms: 'does not accept motion by its essence' means it cannot have motion essentially. Then a longer comment discusses the divine science: place cannot occur except in place, used to argue that God is not spatial. It also notes that the argument can be refuted by considering abstract dimension; the answer to surface proponents applies to dimension proponents. Another comment addresses an objection: the possibility of each part transferring does not imply the possibility of the whole transferring, similar to previous discussions. The difference is that in this case, each part goes to a different place, causing interpenetration. The final incomplete comment discusses the relationship between body and accident regarding motion.

الوجه الثاني
البعد
التسلسل
المكان
الإلهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the second argument is just a repetition of the first, but it is a separate proof based on the nature of place requiring a locus.
Correction: The second argument focuses on the regress problem, not on motion.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument claim would happen if place were a dimension?
A) Place would be immaterial.
B) Place would require another place, leading to infinite regress.
C) Bodies would not be able to move.
D) God would be spatial.

Key Takeaway
If place were a dimension, it would need another place to exist, leading to an unacceptable infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If infinite regress is impossible, what does that imply about your own reliance on chains of reasoning that never end? Are there areas in your faith where you seek endless justifications instead of resting in God's ultimate causality?
?
This argument uses a logical impossibility to reject a false view. When you encounter a belief that leads to absurd consequences, are you willing to abandon it, or do you cling to it despite the logical contradiction?

Refutation of Place as a Moving Dimension via Infinite Regress
Sanusi refutes place as moving dimension using infinite regress and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam argument uses infinite regress and contradiction to refute the view that place is a moving dimension.

Core Idea
If place moves, it leads to an infinite chain of places and a logical contradiction.

Theologians debated whether 'place' is a real dimension that can move. This passage refutes that notion by showing that if place were a moving dimension, it would require another place for itself, leading to an endless chain. Furthermore, the collective movement of all these places results in a contradiction. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the argument starts with an assumption and then draws consequences.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The passage begins with the view that place is a dimension (bu'd) that can undergo locative motion (haraka ayniyya). This is the starting point of the refutation.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If place is a moving dimension, it must have a place itself, which is also a dimension. That place can move, requiring another place, and so on infinitely. This leads to an infinite number of nested dimensions.
The term 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) is key.
4
Understand the impossibility
The infinite regress is declared impossible by necessity (muhal bil-darura). The next part of the passage strengthens this by showing a contradiction.
Note that the impossibility is asserted before the contradiction argument.
5
Analyze the contradiction
If all those places could move (since each can), then the whole set would move, requiring a place that is both inside (as one of them) and outside (as container) – a contradiction (khalf). This proves the initial assumption false.
This is a standard kalam technique: reducing an opponent's view to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكان إلى مكان آخر فله أي لذلك البعد الذي هو المكان مكان آخر هو بعد أيضاً. وينقل الكلام إليه بأنه يقبل الحركة الأينية فله مكان ثالث ويتسلسل فيكون هناك أبعاد غير متناهية متداخلة بعضها في بعض. وأنه محال بالضرورة. وكيف لا يكون محالاً وجميع تلك الأمكنة من حيث هي جميع يمكن انتقاله لأنه إذا أمكن انتقال كل واحد منها أمكن انتقال الكل من حيث هو كل أيضاً، ألا ترى أنه إذا خرج كل واحد عن مكانه فقد خرج الكل. فله أي للجميع مكان فذلك المكان داخل في تلك الأمكنة لأنه أحدها وخارج عنها لأنه ظرف لها، هذا خلف لأنه جمع بين النقيضين.
From place to another place: so it (i.e., that dimension which is place) has another place which is also a dimension. And the discourse is transferred to it because it admits locative motion, so it has a third place, and it goes on ad infinitum, resulting in infinite dimensions interpenetrating each other. And that is impossible by necessity. How could it not be impossible, while all those places, insofar as they are all, it is possible for them to transfer? For if the transfer of each one of them is possible, then the transfer of the whole insofar as it is a whole is also possible. Do you not see that if each one leaves its place, then the whole has left? So the whole has a place, and that place is inside those places because it is one of them, and outside them because it is a container for them. This is a contradiction because it combines two opposites.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that place is a dimension that can move. The argument proceeds as follows: If place is a dimension that admits locative motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya), then that dimension itself must be in a place, leading to a third place, and so on ad infinitum. This infinite regress is impossible by necessity. Moreover, if all those places could move (since each individual can), then the whole set of places would also move, and then that whole would require a place that is both inside the set (as one of the places) and outside it (as container), which is a contradiction. Thus the initial assumption is false.

Key Terms
المكان (al-makan)
البعد (al-bu'd)
الحركة الأينية (al-haraka al-ayniyya)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
الخلف (al-khalf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to physical places, not to the concept of place as a dimension.
Correction: The argument targets the very definition of place as a dimension that can move; it is not limited to physical places but to the logical category.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the first refutation against the view that place is a moving dimension?
A) Place cannot be a dimension because dimensions are eternal.
B) If place is a moving dimension, it leads to an infinite regress and a contradiction, so it is impossible.
C) Place is a non-moving dimension that contains bodies.
D) The body itself is a place for accidents.

Key Takeaway
The view that place is a moving dimension leads to an infinite regress and a logical contradiction, proving its impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions in your own life have you never examined for logical consistency? Will you take time this week to trace one of them to its roots like Sanusi does here?
?
If you believe something about your spiritual state (e.g., that you are 'fine' with Allah), does that belief hold up when you consider its implications? Or does it lead to a contradiction you have ignored?
?
Sanusi shows that a false premise leads to absurdity. Is there a belief you cling to that, if honestly pursued to its end, would force you to admit it is false? What would it cost you to let it go?

Motion Impossibility Refutation
Refutes the view that place is a non-moving dimension by showing it would prevent all body motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that place is a non-moving dimension logically implies that no body can move, and why observable motion disproves that claim.

Core Idea
If place were a fixed dimension, no body could move—but bodies do move.

Sanusi now presents a second refutation against the view that place is a non-moving dimension. Using a logical conditional, he shows that if the dimension itself cannot move, then any body containing it also cannot move. But we see bodies moving every day. This contradiction refutes the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the use of conditional structure (لو) and the terms البعد, الحركة, اللازم, الملزوم.
The passage is a single logical argument, not a list.
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the conditional: if place is a non-moving dimension (P), then the body also cannot move (Q). Then note the observation: bodies do move (not Q). Therefore the original claim must be false (not P).
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on the role of observation
Observe how empirical evidence (المشاهدة) is used to deny the consequent. Without observation, the argument would only be hypothetical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أنه لو كان المكان هو البعد فلأن البعد إذا لم يقبل الحركة لذاته فالجسم أيضاً لا يقبلها لما فيه من البعد فإن حركة الجسم مستلزمة لحركة البعد الحال فيه فامتناع حركة البعد مستلزم لامتناع حركة الجسم واللازم وهو عدم قبول الجسم الحركة باطل بالمشاهدة الدالة على قبوله إياها فكذا الملزوم وهو عدم قبول البعد للحركة باطل.
The second aspect: that if place were the dimension, then because the dimension does not accept motion in itself, the body also would not accept it due to the dimension in it. For the motion of the body necessitates the motion of the dimension inhering in it. Thus the impossibility of the motion of the dimension entails the impossibility of the motion of the body. And the consequent, which is the body not accepting motion, is false by observation which indicates that it accepts it. Thus the antecedent, which is the dimension not accepting motion, is also false.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation: the opponent might claim that place is a dimension that does not accept motion in itself. The argument states that if the dimension itself cannot move, then any body containing that dimension also cannot move, because the body's motion would require the motion of the dimension inhering in it. But we observe that bodies do move, so the consequent is false. Therefore the antecedent (that the dimension does not accept motion) is also false. This refutes the view that place is a non-moving dimension.

Key Terms
الحركة (al-haraka)
البعد (al-bu'd)
اللازم (al-lazim)
الملزوم (al-malzum)
المشاهدة (al-mushahada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes that bodies move to disprove the definition of place.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it is a valid modus tollens. It does not assume the definition is false; instead, it shows that the definition leads to a contradiction with observed fact, thereby disproving the definition.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the second refutation?
A) If place is a non-moving dimension, then bodies cannot move; but bodies do move; therefore place is not a non-moving dimension.
B) If place is a moving dimension, then infinite regress occurs; therefore place is not a moving dimension.
C) Bodies move because of an external mover; place is irrelevant.
D) Dimensions are mental constructs, not real.

Key Takeaway
The observation of bodily motion disproves the claim that place is a non-moving dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that observable reality (المشاهدة) can dismantle a logically reasoned but false claim. Is there an idea you hold onto even though your own life experience contradicts it? How long will you ignore the evidence before you let it go?
?
The argument shows that if place were a static dimension, you could not move a single limb. Every time you move, you witness the falsity of that claim. What does this teach you about the importance of grounding your beliefs in what is actually true, not just what seems plausible?
?
This lesson uses a proof to refute a wrong concept. When you discover a fault in your own understanding, do you cling to comfort or seek the truth? What will you do today to submit to evidence over pride?

Impossibility of Overlapping Dimensions in Place
Sanusi refutes the view that place is a dimension by proving interpenetration is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why place cannot be a dimension because the interpenetration of dimensions leads to an impossibility.

Core Idea
Dimensions cannot interpenetrate, so place cannot be a dimension.

Sanusi launches a decisive refutation against the view that place is a dimension. He argues that if place were a dimension, then when a body occupies a place, its dimension would have to penetrate the place's dimension. This leads to self-evident contradictions, proving that place must be something other than a dimension.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow and key terms.
Notice the logical structure: Sanusi considers three scenarios regarding the existence of the two dimensions.
2
Analyze the three logical possibilities
Identify the three scenarios: both dimensions non-existent, one non-existent, both existent. Understand why each scenario leads to a contradiction.
The only viable scenario (both existent) leads to interpenetration, which is deemed impossible.
3
Examine the reductio ad absurdum
Focus on the mustard seed example: if interpenetration were allowed, the entire world could fit into a mustard seed. This shows the absurdity of the assumption.
This is a classic argument by reduction to absurdity.
4
Understand the general principle
Sanusi extends the argument: the impossibility of interpenetration is a property of any extended entity (dimension), whether material or abstract. Thus, the argument is universal.
This counters a potential misunderstanding that the argument only applies to physical dimensions.
5
Complete the checkpoint and activity
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question and the activity that asks you to reconstruct the argument.
The activity will help solidify the logical steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معاً وللجسم بعد حال فيه فإذا حصل الجسم في المكان نفذ بعد الجسم في البعد الذي هو المكان إذ لا يجوز أن يعدم البعدان معاً حال كونه حاصلاً فيه وإلا كان المتمكن المعدوم بانعدام لازمه حاصلاً في مكان معدوم، ولا أن يعدم أحدهما وإلا كان المتمكن الموجود في مكان معدوم أو بالعكس. وإذا كان البعدان موجودين نفذ أحدهما في الآخر، فيجتمع في الجسم بعدان متداخلان وأنه محال بالضرورة لأن كل بعدين فهما لا محالة أكثر من أحدهما وتداخل المقادير من حيث إنها موصوفة بالعظم بديهي الاستحالة. ولو جاز تداخل البعدين بحيث يصيران متحدين في الإشارة الحسية لجاز تداخل العالم في حيز خردلة وهو باطل بالبديهة. وأيضاً فإنه أي امتناع التداخل حكم ثبت للمتحيز بذاته وهو البعد لأنه ممتد بذاته في الجهات فلا بد له من حيز ومكان يشغله على انفراده دون المادة إذ لا مدخل لها في اقتضاء الحيز وامتناع التداخل فلا يجوز تداخل البعدين مطلقاً سواء كانا ماديين أو مجردين أو مختلفين.
Together, and the body has a dimension inhering in it. So when the body occurs in the place, the dimension of the body penetrates into the dimension which is the place. For it is not permissible that both dimensions be non-existent while it is occurring in it; otherwise the located thing would be non-existent due to the non-existence of its necessary concomitant, occurring in a non-existent place. Nor is it permissible that one of them be non-existent, otherwise the located existent thing would be in a non-existent place, or vice versa. And if both dimensions exist, one penetrates the other. Thus two interpenetrating dimensions would be combined in the body, and that is impossible by necessity. For any two dimensions are necessarily more than one of them, and interpenetration of magnitudes insofar as they are described as greatness is self-evidently impossible. And if the interpenetration of two dimensions were permissible such that they become united in sensory indication, then it would be permissible for the world to interpenetrate into the space of a mustard seed, which is self-evidently false. Moreover, the impossibility of interpenetration is a judgment established for what is extended in itself (al-mutahayyiz bi-dhatihi), which is the dimension, because it is extended in itself in directions; so it must have its own space and place that it occupies exclusively, independently of matter, for matter has no role in requiring space and the impossibility of interpenetration. Thus it is not permissible for two dimensions to interpenetrate absolutely, whether they are material, abstract, or different.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that place is a dimension by demonstrating the impossibility of overlapping dimensions. The argument: when a body enters a place, the body's dimension and the place's dimension must both exist; otherwise, the body would be in a non-existent place or the place would be non-existent. If both exist, one must penetrate the other, resulting in two dimensions in the same body—a self-evident impossibility. If interpenetration were allowed, then the entire world could fit into a mustard seed, which is absurd. Furthermore, the impossibility of interpenetration is a property of any entity extended in itself (such as a dimension), whether material or immaterial. Thus, place cannot be a dimension.

Key Terms
البعد (al-bu'd)
التداخل (al-tadakhul)
المتحيز بالذات (al-mutahayyiz bi-dhatihi)
المتمكن (al-mutamakkin)
الإشارة الحسية (al-ishara al-hissiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument only applies to physical (material) dimensions, not to abstract or immaterial ones.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the impossibility of interpenetration holds for any dimension, whether material, abstract, or different, because it is a property of being extended in itself.

Checkpoint
What does the 'mustard seed' example illustrate in the third refutation?
A) That dimensions are limited and cannot expand infinitely.
B) That if interpenetration were possible, the entire world could fit into a tiny space, which is absurd.
C) That mustard seeds are indivisible particles.
D) That place is not a dimension but a container.

Key Takeaway
Because interpenetration of dimensions is impossible, place cannot be a dimension, and any extended entity must occupy its own exclusive space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all dimensions, including the space you occupy, are exclusive and cannot interpenetrate, how does this shift your understanding of the angels' presence in the same room as you? Do you now realize that your physical existence demands a unique 'space' that even spiritual beings cannot share? How will you use this exclusivity to fulfill your purpose in the place God has assigned you?
?
Sanusi proves that matter has no role in requiring space; it is a property of any extended entity. Consider the 'space' in your heart for faith, love, and devotion. Can you allow worldly concerns and spiritual aspirations to interpenetrate? Or must one fully occupy? What needs to be removed from your heart to make exclusive space for what truly matters?
?
The argument against interpenetration leads to a necessary impossibility. Reflect on a habit or belief in your life that you have allowed to 'overlap' with your faith — something you thought was harmless but actually occupies the same space as your commitment to God. What specific step will you take today to remove that contradiction?

Place and the Impossibility of Dimensional Interpenetration
Defines place and proves impossibility of dimensional interpenetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of place in kalam as continuous quantity and the first rational proof that two dimensions cannot interpenetrate in one body.

Core Idea
Two dimensions cannot coexist in one body; that is self-evident and leads to absurdities.

Before we can discuss how bodies occupy space, we need a clear definition of place. The kalam tradition defines place as a continuous quantity, and from this definition emerges a powerful proof that two dimensions cannot interpenetrate. This passage establishes the logical foundation for understanding spatial relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author defines place and then builds an argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: al-makan, al-bu'd, al-tadakhul.
2
Identify the definition of place
Find where the author states that place is a type of continuous quantity. Understand that a body has a dimension (bu'd) inherent in it.
3
Follow the proof steps
The author argues that when a body enters a place, its dimension must penetrate the place's dimension. He eliminates the possibilities of both dimensions ceasing or one ceasing, concluding both must exist and interpenetrate.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the self-evident absurdity
The author asserts that interpenetration of magnitudes is self-evidently impossible, and illustrates with the example of the world fitting into a mustard seed.
This shows the conclusion is false, so the premise must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل. وللجسم بعد حال فيه فإذا حصل الجسم في المكان نفذ بعد الجسم في البعد الذي هو المكان إذ لا يجوز أن يعدم البعدان معاً حال كونه حاصلاً فيه وإلا كان المتمكن المعدوم بانعدام لازمه حاصلاً في مكان معدوم، ولا أن يعدم أحدهما وإلا كان المتمكن الموجود في مكان معدوم أو بالعكس، وإذا كان البعدان موجودين نفذ أحدهما في الآخر، فيجتمع في الجسم بعدان متداخلان، وأنه محال بالضرورة لأن كل بعدين فهما لا محالة أكثر من أحدهما وتداخل المقادير من حيث إنها موصوفة بالعظم بديهي الاستحالة سواء كانت ذلك موجباً للاتحاد ورفع التعدد في نفس الأمر أو للاتحاد في الوضع وقبول الإشارة. ولو جاز تداخل البعدين بحيث يصيران متحدين في الإشارة الحسية لجاز تداخل العالم في حيز خردلة بأن ينقطع قطعة قطعة على مقدار خردلة خردلة ثم يتداخل كلها في واحدة منها وهو باطل بالبديهة.
The second observatory - the ninth topic: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. A body has a dimension inherent in it. When the body enters the place, the body's dimension penetrates into the dimension which is the place, because it is not permissible that both dimensions cease to exist while it is present in it, otherwise the located (al-mutamakkin) would be non-existent due to the non-existence of its necessary concomitant while being present in a non-existent place, nor that one of them ceases to exist, otherwise the existing located would be in a non-existent place or vice versa. And if the two dimensions exist, one of them penetrates the other, so there come together in the body two interpenetrating dimensions, and that is necessarily impossible, because any two dimensions are inevitably more than one of them, and the interpenetration of magnitudes insofar as they are described as greatness is self-evidently impossible, whether that necessitates union and removal of multiplicity in actual fact or union in position and being subject to indication. And if interpenetration of two dimensions were permissible such that they become one in sensory indication, then it would be permissible for the world to interpenetrate the space of a mustard seed by being cut piece by piece into the size of a mustard seed and then all interpenetrate into one of them, and that is false by self-evidence.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-makan' (place) and presents the first proof for the impossibility of dimensional interpenetration. The author begins by stating that place is a type of continuous quantity. He explains that a body has a dimension (bu'd) inherent in it. When a body enters a place, its dimension must penetrate the dimension that is the place. The author then argues that it is impossible for both dimensions to cease to exist while the body is present, because then the located entity would be non-existent due to the non-existence of its necessary concomitant (i.e., its dimension) while being present in a non-existent place. Similarly, it is impossible for only one dimension to cease, because then an existing located entity would be in a non-existent place or vice versa. Therefore, both dimensions must exist, and one must penetrate the other, resulting in two interpenetrating dimensions in one body. This, the author asserts, is necessarily impossible because any two dimensions are inevitably greater than one. The interpenetration of magnitudes described as 'greatness' (al-'uthm) is self-evidently impossible, whether it leads to numerical unity in reality or merely union in position and being subject to sensory indication. To illustrate, he gives a reductio ad absurdum: if two dimensions could interpenetrate to become one in sensory indication, then it would be possible for the entire world to be compressed into the space of a mustard seed, which is patently absurd.

Correction: The author shows that interpenetration leads to self-evident absurdities (e.g., world in mustard seed), proving impossibility by reason, not definition.

The Second Proof: Impenetrability Belongs to Dimension, Not Matter
Second proof: Impenetrability is a property of the dimension itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of interpenetration is a necessary property of the dimension itself, independent of matter.

Core Idea
Impenetrability is a property of dimension, not matter.

In this second proof, the author deepens the argument by showing that the impossibility of interpenetration is not due to matter but to the very nature of dimension. This is a subtle but crucial point in kalam physics. Let's read the passage carefully to see how he establishes this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: al-mutahayyiz bi-dhatih (self-subsistent spatial entity) and al-bu'd (dimension).
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that the impossibility of interpenetration is a judgment (hukm) established for the self-subsistent spatial entity itself, which is the dimension. Matter plays no role.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The author adds a conditional: if interpenetration of dimensions were permissible, then the world could fit into a mustard seed. This absurdity shows the necessity of impenetrability.
This is a typical kalam reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the role of matter
Consider why matter is irrelevant: it is the dimension's self-extension that requires exclusive space, not any material property.
This distinguishes the kalam view from Aristotelian physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه أي امتناع التداخل حكم ثبت للمتحيز بالذات وهو البعد لأنه ممتد بذاته في الجهات فلا بد له من حيز ومكان يشغله على انفراده دون المادة إذ لا مدخل لها في اقتضاء الحيز وامتناع التداخل فلا يجوز تداخل البعدين مطلقاً سواء كانا ماديين أو مجردين أو مختلفين. وقد فقد في بعض النسخ لفظة وأيضاً وعلى هذا يكون قوله فإنه بياناً للشرطية أي لو جاز تداخل البعدين لجاز تداخل العالم في حيز خردلة لأن امتناع التداخل المعلوم في الأجسام حكم ثبت للمتحيز بالذات إذ يجب أن يكون كل من المتحيزين بالذات منفرداً بحيز على حدة.
And also, verily it (i.e., the impossibility of interpenetration) is a judgment established for the self-subsistent spatial entity (al-mutahayyiz bi-dhatih), which is the dimension (al-bu'd), because it is extended by itself in the directions, so it must have a space and place that it occupies exclusively, without matter, for matter has no role in necessitating space and the impossibility of interpenetration. So interpenetration of two dimensions is absolutely not permissible, whether they are material, abstract, or different. And in some copies the word 'and also' is missing, and accordingly his statement 'verily it' is an explanation of the conditional, i.e., if interpenetration of two dimensions were permissible, then interpenetration of the world into the space of a mustard seed would be permissible, because the impossibility of interpenetration known in bodies is a judgment established for the self-subsistent spatial entity, since each of the two self-subsistent spatial entities must be alone in a separate space.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof for the impossibility of dimensional interpenetration. The author states that the impossibility of interpenetration is a judgment (hukm) that belongs to the self-subsistent spatial entity (al-mutahayyiz bi-dhatih), which is the dimension (al-bu'd). The dimension is extended by itself in all directions, so it necessarily requires a unique space (hayyiz) and place (makan) that it occupies exclusively. Matter (al-madda) has no role in necessitating space or the impossibility of interpenetration. Therefore, interpenetration is absolutely impossible for any two dimensions, whether they are material (madiyyan), abstract (mujarradan), or different (mukhtalifan). The author notes that in some manuscript copies the word 'and also' is missing, and in that case the phrase 'verily it' explains the conditional: if interpenetration were allowed, then the world could fit into a mustard seed, because the known impossibility of interpenetration in bodies is a judgment belonging to the self-subsistent spatial entity, and each such entity must have its own separate space.

Third Proof: Interpenetration Undermines Personal Identity
The author argues that interpenetration leads to skepticism about identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against interpenetration: that it would destroy confidence in individual identity and lead to manifest sophistry.

Core Idea
If interpenetration were possible, you couldn't trust that one is one and not many.

The author now presents a practical argument: if bodies could interpenetrate, then we could not be sure that anything is a single, distinct entity. This would upend basic self-evident truths (badihiyyat) and open the door to full-blown skepticism (safsata). Read the passage to see how this reductio ad absurdum unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then consult the literal English translation. Notice the two examples given: a cubit and a person.
The passage is concise but packed with implications.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the premise (permission of interpenetration) and the absurd consequences (one cubit becomes many; one person becomes many). Ask why these consequences are absurd.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that trust in self-evident truths vanishes and this leads to sophistry. Consider what 'sophistry' means in this context.
Safsata refers to skepticism that denies obvious facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه أي تجويز التداخل بين الأبعاد يرفع الأمان عن الوحدة الشخصية ويقدح في الوثوق بها فإنه يجوز على تقدير جواز التداخل كون هذا الذراع المعين المشخص ذراعين بل أذرعاً كثيرة ويجوز على تقديره أيضاً كون شخص واحد من الإنسان شخصين بل أشخاصاً متعددة فيرتفع الوثوق عن أمثال هذه البديهيات، وأنه سفسطة ظاهرة.
And also, verily it (i.e., the permission of interpenetration between dimensions) removes security from personal unity (al-wahda al-shakhsiyya) and impairs trust in it, for it is possible, on the assumption that interpenetration is permissible, that this specific, particularized cubit be two cubits, nay, many cubits, and it is also possible on that assumption that one single person be two persons, nay, multiple persons. Thus trust in such self-evident truths (al-badihiyyat) vanishes, and that is manifest sophistry (safsata).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against dimensional interpenetration: it would undermine the certainty of personal identity (al-wahda al-shakhsiyya). The author argues that if interpenetration were allowed, then a specific, individual cubit (dhira' mu'ayyan mushakhkhas) could be two cubits or many cubits. Similarly, one single human person could be two persons or multiple persons. This would destroy our trust in self-evident truths (al-badihiyyat) such as the identity of an individual. The author concludes that this would be manifest sophistry (safsata), i.e., a form of skepticism that denies obvious facts.

Fourth Proof: Interpenetration Entails Conjoining of Similar Things
Argument that interpenetration would necessitate two similar things in one matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how interpenetration of dimensions would necessitate the impossible conjoining of two similar things, a principle already refuted in kalam.

Core Idea
Interpenetration forces two similar dimensions to coexist in one matter, which is rationally impossible.

This passage presents the fourth argument against the atomist theory of interpenetration. The author argues that if two dimensions interpenetrate, they would be similar and occupy the same matter, leading to the conjoining of two similar things (ijtima' al-mithlayn)—an impossibility already established earlier. This proof relies on the principle that two identical essences cannot be distinguished without a distinct location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud and its literal English translation. Notice the logical connector 'فإن' which introduces the causal link.
The passage is concise but packed with technical terms.
2
Identify the key claim
Locate the claim that interpenetration (tadakhul) leads to the conjoining of two similar things (ijtima' al-mithlayn). Ask: What are the two similar things here? They are the two dimensions.
The term 'mithlayn' means two things that are identical in essence.
3
Understand the impossibility
Reflect on why two similar dimensions cannot coexist in one matter. In kalam, two similar things are indistinguishable unless separated by location; in one matter they would be identical and thus one, not two. The author says this was already refuted.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum: interpenetration implies an impossibility, so interpenetration is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه يلزم على تقدير تداخل البعدين اجتماع المثلين فإن ذينك البعدين متماثلان قد اجتمعا في مادة واحدة وقد أبطلناه فيما سبق.
And also, verily it follows, on the assumption of interpenetration of two dimensions, the conjoining of two similar things (ijtima' al-mithlayn), for those two dimensions are similar and have come together in one matter, and we have already refuted that previously.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof: if two dimensions interpenetrate, then they would be two similar things (mithlayn) coexisting in one matter. The author states that this is impossible because it leads to the conjoining of two similar things (ijtima' al-mithlayn), which has already been refuted earlier in the work. The argument relies on the principle that two similar things cannot occupy the same subject simultaneously.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think two similar dimensions can coexist in the same matter because they are not identical in all respects (e.g., one could be longer, the other shorter).
Correction: In kalam, dimensions are considered similar in essence (mihamyan). Two similar essences cannot be differentiated except by location; in the same matter, they would be one, not two. The conjoining of two similar things is impossible because it would require them to be two and one simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Interpenetration of dimensions is impossible because it would require two similar things to occupy the same matter, which is rationally absurd.

Reflect on one desire or priority that has 'interpenetrated' your worship. Make a concrete plan to separate it today, so your heart's matter is pure for Allah alone.

The Two Types of Dimensions: Refuting the Objection
The author responds to the first objection by distinguishing between accidental and self-subsistent dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author distinguishes between accidental and self-subsistent dimensions to refute the objection that bodies would be motionless if the dimension of place cannot move.

Core Idea
Dimensions are not all alike—some are inherent in matter, others self-subsistent.

The objection claimed that if the dimension of place cannot move, then bodies, which also have a dimension, cannot move either. The author responds by rejecting the assumption that all dimensions are alike. Instead, he distinguishes between the dimension that is accidental to matter and the dimension that is self-subsistent. Only if they were similar would the necessity hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author structures his answer.
The passage is a direct response to an objection, so pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the objection and the author's initial stance
The objection is stated within the author's answer. Find where the objector's claim is quoted, and note the author's response: 'we choose that the dimension which is the place does not admit local motion.'
3
Understand the distinction between dimensions
The author denies the necessity by pointing out that the dimension in a body is 'subsistent in matter' (accidental) while the dimension of place is 'self-subsistent'. Recognise how this distinction breaks the supposed link.
The Arabic terms used are 'القائم بالمادة' and 'القائم بنفسه'.
4
Reflect on the condition of similarity
The author says the necessity would only follow if the dimensions were similar (تماثل). Ask yourself: why does similarity matter? Because only if they were identical in nature would the property of motionlessness transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الوجه الأول أنا نختار أن البعد الذي هو المكان لا يقبل الحركة الأينية. قوله فلا يقبلها الجسم أيضاً لما فيه من البعد قلنا: هذا اللزوم ممنوع إذ البعد الذي في الجسم قائم بالمادة حال فيها والبعد الذي فيه الجسم أعني قائم بنفسه غير حال في المادة وإنهما مختلفان بالحقيقة فلا يلزم حينئذ من عدم قبول أحدهما الحركة عدم قبول الآخر إياها، إنما يلزم ذلك على تقدير التماثل في
The answer to the first objection is that we choose that the dimension which is the place does not admit local motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya). [Objector's] statement: 'Hence the body also does not admit it because it contains a dimension.' We say: This necessity is denied, for the dimension that is in the body is subsistent in matter, inherent in it, while the dimension in which the body is, i.e., the one that is self-subsistent, is not inherent in matter, and they are different in reality. So it does not follow then from the non-acceptance of motion by one of them the non-acceptance by the other; that would only follow on the assumption of similarity (al-tamathul) in...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first objection raised earlier. The objection was: if the dimension of place does not accept motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya), then bodies, which contain a dimension, also would not accept motion, leading to the impossibility of motion. The author responds by stating that he chooses the position that the dimension which is the place (al-bu'd alladhi huwa al-makan) does not admit local motion. He then refutes the claim that this necessity (luzum) applies: the dimension in the body is subsistent in matter (qa'im bi al-madda) and inherent in it, while the dimension that is place is self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsih) and not inherent in matter. These two types of dimensions are different in reality (mukhtalifan bi al-haqiqa). Therefore, the fact that one does not accept motion does not imply that the other also does not accept it. The necessity would only follow if the two dimensions were similar (tamathul), but they are not.

Key Terms
البعد المكاني
الحركة الأينية
القائم بالمادة
القائم بنفسه
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one dimension does not move, then all dimensions (including those in bodies) do not move.
Correction: The author distinguishes between accidental dimensions (in matter) and self-subsistent dimensions (place). They are different in reality, so motionlessness of one does not transfer to the other.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny the necessity (luzum) that bodies would also be motionless?
A) He agrees that bodies do not move.
B) He claims that the dimension in the body is accidental, while the dimension of place is self-subsistent, so they are different.
C) He argues that motion is an illusion.
D) He says that the objection is invalid because the dimension of place does move.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that the dimension of place and the dimension in a body are fundamentally different, so motionlessness of one does not imply the other.

Place as a Single or Composite Surface
Al-Jurjani describes two types of surfaces for place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first implication of the surface theory: that place can be a single surface or composed of multiple surfaces.

Core Idea
Place is not always a simple surface—it can be a combination of surfaces.

In this first branch, al-Jurjani explains that if place is a surface, it can be either a single surface surrounding a body, like a bird in the air, or a composite surface, like a stone on the ground where the ground and air both contribute. This sets the stage for further discussions about motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the first branch once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the core idea.
2
Identify the two cases
Notice the two possibilities: place as a single surface (bird in air) versus place as composite (stone on ground). Understand how each fits the definition of place as surface.
The composite surface consists of the ground and air surfaces meeting at the stone's boundary.
3
Connect to the broader theory
Ask yourself why this classification matters: it shows that the surface theory can accommodate both simple and complex placements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول المكان قد يكون سطحاً واحداً كالطير في الهواء) فإن سطحاً واحداً قائماً بالهواء محيط به ( أو أكثر من سطح واحد كالحجر الموضوع على الأرض فإنه ) أي مكانه (أرض (وهواء يعني أنه سطح مركب من سطح الأرض الذي تحته وسطح ) الهواء الذي فوقه ( الثاني) من تلك الفروع ( أنه قد تتحرك السطوح كلها كالسمك الماء الجاري ) فإنه إذا كان في وسط الماء الجاري كان السطح المحيط به سواء فرض واحداً أو مركباً من متعدد متحركاً بتبعية حركة الماء ولما كانت حركة السطح الذي هو المكان بالعرض لا بالذات لم يلزم أن يكون للمكان مكان آخر. ( أو ) يتحرك هو (بعضها كالحجر الموضوع فيه أي في الماء الجاري فإن مكانه مركب من سطح الأرض الساكن وسطح الماء المتحرك . ( أولا) يتحرك أصلاً فيكون المكان ساكناً وهو ظاهر (الثالث) من تلك الفروع ( أنه قد يتحرك الحاوي والمحوي معاً) إما متوافقين في الجهة أو متخالفين فيها ) كالطير يطير والريح تهب ) على الوفاق أو الخلاف (أو) يتحرك (الحاوي وحده كالطير يقف والريح تهب أو) يتحرك ( المحوي وحده كالطير يطير والريح تقف ) . وقد يقال : إذا تحرك الطير: انخرق الهواء قدامه والتأم من خلفه . إذ لا يجوز الخلاء عند أصحاب السطح فيلزم تحرك الهواء من من تحرك الطير فالأولى أن يمثل بكرة تماس بمحدبها مقعر كرة أخرى وبمقعرها محدب كرة ثالثة وتكون المتوسطة متحركة وحدها فيكون مثالاً لكل واحدة من حركتي الحاوي والمحوي وحده
(The first: Place may be a single surface, like a bird in the air) for a single surface subsisting in the air surrounds it. (Or more than one surface, like a stone placed on the ground, for) its place (is ground and air), meaning it is a surface composed of the surface of the ground beneath it and the surface of the air above it. (The second) of those branches (is that all surfaces may move, like a fish in flowing water) for if it is in the middle of flowing water, the surrounding surface, whether considered one or composed of multiple, moves by following the water's movement. And since the movement of the surface which is place is accidental not essential, it does not entail that place has another place. (Or) some of them move (like a stone placed in it, i.e., in flowing water, for its place is composed of the stationary ground surface and the moving water surface. (Or) it does not move at all, so place is stationary, and that is apparent. (The third) of those branches (is that the container and the contained may both move) either in the same direction or in different directions, like a bird flying and the wind blowing, in agreement or opposition. (Or) the container alone moves, like a bird standing still and the wind blowing; (or) the contained alone moves, like a bird flying and the wind standing still. And it may be said: When the bird moves, the air is torn apart in front of it and closes behind it, because vacuum is not allowed for the proponents of the surface view, so the movement of the air is necessitated by the movement of the bird. So it is better to give the example of a sphere whose convex surface touches the concave surface of another sphere, and whose concave surface touches the convex surface of a third sphere, with the middle sphere moving alone, so it is an example for each of the two movements of the container and the contained alone.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three branches that follow from the view that place is a surface. Each branch examines different possibilities for the motion of surfaces relative to the body. The first branch states that place may be a single surface (like a bird's surrounding air) or composed of multiple surfaces (like a stone on the ground: the ground's surface below and the air's surface above). The second branch considers motion: (a) all surfaces may move together, as in a fish in flowing water; the surrounding surface moves accidentally with the water, so place does not require another place. (b) Some surfaces may move, like a stone in flowing water where the ground surface is stationary and the water surface moves. (c) No surfaces move, so place is stationary. The third branch addresses whether the container (the surface) and the contained (the body) move together or separately: (a) both move (bird and wind), (b) only the container moves (bird stationary, wind blowing), (c) only the contained moves (bird flying, wind still). The author then notes a potential objection: if the bird moves, the air is displaced, requiring motion of the air. To avoid this implication, a better example is given using concentric spheres, where the middle sphere moves alone, illustrating independent motion of the contained without requiring the containing surface to move.

Key Terms
السطح
المكان
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first branch, what is the place of a stone placed on the ground?
A) Only the ground surface
B) Only the air surface
C) A composite of ground and air surfaces
D) A single surface of the stone itself

Key Takeaway
Place, if a surface, can be either a single surface (e.g., bird in air) or a composite of multiple surfaces (e.g., stone on ground).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a stone on the ground, do you realize that its 'place' is not just the ground but also the air? How often do you overlook the hidden dimensions of simple things in your life? Will you train yourself to see the layers that actually compose reality?
?
This lesson shows that even something as basic as 'place' has complexity. Does your conceptual laziness extend to matters of faith? Are there aspects of your belief that you treat as single-surface when they are actually composite? What will you do today to explore a deeper aspect of your understanding of Allah's creation?

Motion of Surfaces: All, Some, or None
Al-Jurjani classifies motion possibilities of surfaces composing place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second branch of the surface theory: the three possibilities for the motion of the surfaces constituting place.

Core Idea
The surfaces that make up place can all move, some move, or none move—each with distinct examples.

The second branch examines how the surfaces that constitute place behave when the body is in motion. Al-Jurjani explores three subcases: all surfaces move, some move, or none move, using examples like a fish in flowing water or a stone on a riverbed. He also clarifies why this does not lead to an infinite regress of places.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for the second branch. Pay attention to the three subcases and the reasoning about accidental motion.
2
Identify the three subcases
List them: (a) all surfaces move (fish), (b) some surfaces move (stone in flowing water), (c) no surfaces move (place stationary).
Note the argument that accidental motion avoids requiring a place for place.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification is important: it shows the surface theory can handle dynamic situations without absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fish moves accidentally with the water, not by its own choice. In what areas of your life are you moving by accident—following external currents without intention? What would it take to shift from accidental to essential motion, and what first step will you take today to do so?
?
The stone's place is composed of stable ground and moving water. Consider your own 'place'—your roles, relationships, environment. Which parts are stable and which are shifting? Are you clinging to temporary currents as if they were solid ground? How might distinguishing between essential and accidental help you hold loosely to what changes and firmly to what truly defines you?

Motion of Container and Contained
Al-Jurjani analyzes motion relations between container and contained, ending with a refined model using concentric spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third branch: possibilities for the motion of the container (surface) and the contained (body) together or separately, and the refined example with concentric spheres.

Core Idea
The container and contained can move together, only one can move, and a better example uses nested spheres to avoid problematic implications.

The third branch examines whether the container (the surface) and the contained (the body) move together or independently. Al-Jurjani gives examples like a bird and wind, then addresses a potential objection about air displacement. He proposes a superior example using concentric spheres to illustrate independent motion without requiring the containing surface to move.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for the third branch, including the objection and alternative example. Follow the logic.
2
Identify the three subcases
They are: (a) both move (bird and wind), (b) only container moves (bird still, wind blows), (c) only contained moves (bird flies, wind still).
3
Understand the objection and the spheres example
The objection: if a bird moves, air must tear and close, implying the container moves. The solution: a sphere inside two other spheres, where the middle sphere moves alone, showing the contained can move without the container moving.
This refines the bird example to avoid the necessity of air movement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a bird flies, the surrounding air must move with it, so the container always moves.

?

?

?
